Title: DecodingTrust-Agent Platform (DTap): A Controllable and Interactive Red-Teaming Platform for AI Agents

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.04808v1

Published Time: Thu, 07 May 2026 01:07:35 GMT

Number of Pages: 279

Markdown Content:
# DecodingTrust-Agent Platform (DTap): A Controllable and Interactive Red-Teaming Platform for AI Agents 

Zhaorun Chen 1,2∗ , Xun Liu 3∗, Haibo Tong 3∗, Chengquan Guo 2∗, Yuzhou Nie 1,4∗, Jiawei Zhang 2∗

Mintong Kang 3∗, Chejian Xu 3∗, Qichang Liu 3∗, Xiaogeng Liu 5∗, Tianneng Shi 6∗, Chaowei Xiao 5

Sanmi Koyejo 1,7, Percy Liang 7, Wenbo Guo 1,4, Dawn Song 1,6, Bo Li 1,2,31Virtue AI 2University of Chicago 3University of Illinois, Urbana-Champaign 

> 4

University of California, Santa Barbara 5Johns Hopkins University 

> 6

University of California, Berkeley 7Stanford University 

Abstract 

AI agents are increasingly deployed across diverse domains to automate complex workflows through long-horizon and high-stakes action executions. Due to their high capability and flexibility, such agents raise significant security and safety con-cerns. A growing number of real-world incidents have shown that adversaries can easily manipulate agents into performing harmful actions, such as leaking API keys, deleting user data, or initiating unauthorized transactions. Evaluating agent security is inherently challenging, as agents operate in dynamic, untrusted environments involving external tools, heterogeneous data sources, and frequent user interactions. However, realistic, controllable, and reproducible environments for large-scale risk assessment remain largely underexplored. To address this gap, we introduce the DECODING TRUST -A GENT PLATFORM (DT AP ), the first controllable and interac-tive red-teaming platform for AI agents, spanning 14 real-world domains and over 

50 simulation environments that replicate widely used systems such as Google Workspace, Paypal, and Slack. To scale the risk assessment of agents in DT AP , we further propose DT AP -R ED , the first autonomous red-teaming agent that system-atically explores diverse injection vectors (e.g., prompt, tool, skill, environment, combinations) and autonomously discovers effective attack strategies tailored to varying malicious goals. Using DT AP -R ED , we curate DT AP -B ENCH , a large-scale red-teaming dataset comprising high-quality instances across domains, each paired with a verifiable judge following corresponding security policies to automatically validate attack outcomes. Through DT AP , we conduct large-scale evaluations of popular AI agents built on various backbone models, spanning security policies, risk categories, and attack strategies, revealing systematic vulnerability patterns and providing valuable insights for developing secure next-generation agents. The platform is open-sourced at https://decodingtrust-agent.com .

Figure 1: D ECODING TRUST -A GENT PLATFORM provides a comprehensive security evaluation for AI agents with advanced red-teaming, covering both indirect and direct threat models.

Figure 2: Overview of D ECODING TRUST -A GENT PLATFORM (DT AP ). DT AP is the first con-trollable and interactive simulation platform for advanced red-teaming for AI agents. It spans 14 high-stakes domains (e.g., finance, workflows, coding, customer service) and over 50 widely used environments (e.g., Google Workspace, PayPal, Slack), replicating their real-world counterparts while providing realistic agent interfaces. DT AP supports diverse injection vectors (prompt, tool, skill, environment, and their combinations) and integrates DT AP -R ED , an autonomous red-teaming agent that discovers effective attacks using advanced strategies and feedback from verifiable judges. 

1 Introduction 

Autonomous AI agents are rapidly emerging as a foundational paradigm for real-world intelligent systems, with applications spanning personal productivity (e.g., email and calendar management), enterprise workflows (e.g., CRM and finance operations), and complex decision-making pipelines [ 8,47 , 49 , 37 ]. By enabling large language models (LLMs) to interact with external environments, invoke tools, and maintain persistent memory, agentic systems significantly extend the capabilities of standalone models and push the frontier of AI. However, this increased autonomy and capabilities also introduce fundamentally new security, safety, and privacy risks, which remain a major barrier to their large-scale deployment, particularly in high-stakes domains such as finance, healthcare, legal services, and critical infrastructure [82, 34, 75]. To uncover and mitigate these risks, red-teaming, which systematically probes AI systems with diverse adversarial strategies, has become an essential component of AI safety and security evaluation [ 87 , 36 ,13 ]. While prior work has made progress in red-teaming LLMs, extending these approaches to agentic systems is inherently challenging. Unlike static models, agents operate in dynamic, interactive settings, where they can (i) interact with external environments, (ii) invoke diverse tools with varying semantics, and (iii) leverage specialized harnesses to execute multi-step workflows. Existing agent red-teaming efforts often focus on limited attack surfaces (e.g., direct prompt injection alone) or rely on simplified, static environments, failing to capture the full spectrum of real-world risks [ 29 , 6, 18 , 84 ].Here, a key bottleneck lies in the lack of fully controllable and realistic environments that enable systematic, safe, and reproducible evaluation of agent behaviors under adversarial conditions. In this work, we introduce DECODING TRUST -A GENT PLATFORM (DT AP ), the first fully controlled, comprehensive real-world simulation platform for advanced red-teaming of agentic systems, as illustrated in Fig. 2. Our platform enables head-to-head simulation of over 50 realistic environments across 14 domains, including widely used services such as email (e.g., Gmail), scheduling (e.g., Calendar, Zoom), and financial systems (e.g., PayPal, Robinhood). Specifically, DT AP replicates the official agent-facing MCPs and GUIs of their real-world counterparts and purpose-builds them for red-teaming, enabling transferable security evaluation while maintaining full control and observability over intermediate states and interactions. This design supports fine-grained manipulation and injection of environment payloads, tool supply chains, and contextual inputs, enabling safe evaluation of adversarial scenarios without real-world consequences. By bridging realism and controllability, DT AP provides a scalable foundation for evaluating agent robustness in complex, real-world settings. Building on this platform, we further propose an autonomous red-teaming agent, DT AP -R ED ,equipped with a range of attack skills and strategies, as well as the ability to manipulate the agent’s execution pipeline. Given a victim agent and malicious goals, DT AP -R ED systematically explores 2diverse attack strategies across multiple injection surfaces in an iterative fashion, as illustrated in Fig. 3. Specifically, DT AP -R ED exploits victim agents by jointly optimizing both injection payloads and injection locations across prompt-, tool-, skill-, and environment-level attack vectors, as well as their compositional combinations. In addition, DT AP -R ED leverages verifiable judges that directly inspect outcome states in the environment (e.g., whether an unauthorized transaction has been executed) to determine attack success and provide feedback for unsuccessful attacks to enable iterative improvement. Given the diverse security requirements across applications and regions, we ground our red-teaming in domain-specific risk patterns and derive malicious goals from risk categories systematically extracted from domain policies and regulations (e.g., Salesforce AUP [ 54 ], EU AI Act [ 20 ]) as well as use-case-driven risks. This ensures that each evaluation scenario reflects concrete regulatory requirements and practical risks in enterprise and high-stakes environments. Given the lack of comprehensive and advanced red-teaming benchmarks for AI agents, we build 

DT AP -B ENCH , which covers over 4K diverse malicious goals derived from 300+ risk categories extracted from 60+ security policies across 14 domains. In total, DT AP -B ENCH includes 6,682 high-quality tasks, of which 3,876 are red-teaming tasks, each paired with a manually designed verifiable judge to assess task completion or attack success. Specifically, we employ DT AP -R ED 

to optimize effective attacks for each malicious goal, followed by human review to ensure their quality and practicality, with the distribution of tasks and injection types shown in Fig. 4. To evaluate the trade-off between security and utility, DT AP -B ENCH includes both a benign task set for utility evaluation and two separate red-teaming task sets under two threat models: (1) indirect threat model ,where a benign user issues a legitimate query while a third-party adversary introduces a hidden malicious goal via indirect injection vectors (e.g., environment injection, MCP tool poisoning, or skill injection); and (2) direct threat model , where the user themselves acts as the attacker and directly issues malicious queries, potentially combined with indirect injections, to induce harmful actions and misuse agents against other users or systems. We conduct extensive experiments across a wide range of state-of-the-art agentic frameworks on DT AP -B ENCH , including OpenClaw, Claude Code, Google ADK, and OpenAI Agents SDK, with ad-vanced backbone models such as GPT-5.5, Gemini-3-Pro, Claude-Sonnet-4.5, as well as open-source models such as DeepSeek-V4-Pro. As shown in Fig. 1, all agents exhibit substantial vulnerability under advanced red-teaming attacks. Overall, Google ADK is the most vulnerable under the indirect threat model (55.7% ASR), while OpenClaw (DeepSeek-V4-Pro) exhibits the highest ASR under direct threat model (59.6%). Even the most robust agent, Claude Code, still reaches over 25.2% ASR, indicating that security remains a major challenge for current agentic frameworks and highlighting the effectiveness of DT AP -B ENCH . Our key findings are as follows: • Asymmetric vulnerability across indirect injection surfaces. Skill- and tool-level injections consistently achieve higher ASR than environment injections, suggesting that environment inputs are treated as more external, while internalized channels remain highly exploitable. This asymmetry also varies across frameworks: OpenClaw shows substantially lower ASR on tool injections than OpenAI Agents and Google ADK, indicating its strong trust calibration against external plugins. • Compositional injections are highly effective. Agents are particularly vulnerable to compositional attacks that combine multiple injection vectors, such as email thread fabrication, multi-message contextual injections, multi-step attack chains, and multimodal typographic injections (see Tab. 1). • Uneven susceptibility to direct and indirect attacks. OpenAI Agents, Claude Code, and Open-Claw are more vulnerable to direct prompt injections than indirect, indicating strong reliance on user instructions. In contrast, Gemini-based agents show lower ASR under direct attacks, suggesting weaker instruction-following but improved robustness to explicit malicious prompts. • Prompt-level guardrails are insufficient. While agents such as Claude Code and OpenClaw reduce ASR through prompt-level defenses, these protections are limited, and sophisticated attacks can still succeed. Strong security protection requires additional harness-level designs (e.g., external guardrails or by-design execution controls). • Context-aware risks dominate. Agents are more vulnerable to risks that require contextual reasoning and information flow analysis over multi-step action execution (e.g., Data Exfiltration ,

Sensitive Data Handling , Privilege Escalation ), whereas relatively robust against content-generation risks (e.g., Sexual Content , Weaponization ), potentially due to model-level alignment. • Environment-dependent vulnerability. Communication-centric environments (e.g., Gmail, What-sApp, Calendar) are significantly more susceptible due to their rich and dynamic interactions, while 3Table 1: Red-teaming examples with severe consequences from DT AP -B ENCH demonstrating diverse injection vectors and sophisticated attack strategies across environments and risk categories.

finance-related environments such as PayPal, E-Commerce, and travel booking exhibit lower ASR, with agents often ignoring even obvious injections in tool descriptions due to their sensitive nature. • Open-source agent backbones are more susceptible to direct misuse. Agents built on open-source models such as DeepSeek-V4-Pro exhibit the highest ASR under the direct threat model, indicating strong instruction-following but weaker ability to distinguish malicious intent. In contrast, closed-source backbones are much more aligned against such direct attacks. • Execute-then-refuse failure mode. OpenAI Agents SDK and Google ADK frequently execute harmful actions before issuing refusals, likely due to batch tool invocation that hampers per-tool consequence reasoning, which significantly contributes to their high ASR. In summary, we make the following key contributions: • A comprehensive and fully controllable red-teaming platform, DT AP , spanning 50+ simulated, full-stack environments with faithful MCP and GUI replication of their real-world counterparts, purpose-built for agent utility and security evaluation. • An autonomous red-teaming agent, DT AP -R ED , equipped with 200+ sophisticated attack strate-gies that systematically explore attacks across diverse injection vectors (prompt, tool, skill, and environment, and their combinations). • A policy-grounded red-teaming benchmark, DT AP -B ENCH , comprising 6,682 tasks and covering 4K+ malicious goals from 300+ risk categories across 14 domains, each paired with effective attacks optimized against a surrogate agent and a verifiable judge that checks environment states. • We invest substantial human and compute resources to build the comprehensive evaluation platform and high-quality benchmark, including over 16,000 expert hours from a team of 17 red-teaming specialists across 20 months, and $120,000 in API credits for data curation and validation. • Extensive evaluations across frontier agent frameworks and models reveal novel and insightful findings regarding their security, offering meaningful guidance for building more secure agents. 42 Related Works 

2.1 Red-Teaming Environments and Simulators for AI Agents 

Prior work on red-teaming environments for AI agents remains limited in both realism and coverage. For example, AgentDojo [ 18 ] and AgentHarm [ 6] lack dynamic environments and rely on static, synthetic tools with injections embedded in hardcoded outputs, which fail to capture the risks arising from realistic agent dynamics. Similarly, SHADE-Arena [ 31 ] and Petri [ 23 ] introduce more interactive environments, but these remain highly simplified and cannot faithfully model the diversity of real-world adversaries encountered by agents. On the other hand, ST-4 [ 33 ], RedTeamCUA [ 35 ], SafeArena [ 60 ], and ShieldAgent [ 12 ] aim to evaluate agents in more realistic settings, but are limited to a small number of environments and narrow domains, and often lack key design properties such as flexible state resets and deterministic transitions required for reproducible red-teaming. In contrast, DT AP includes 50+ full-stack environments purpose-built for red-teaming that replicate their agent interfaces and support high-concurrency evaluation and resetting to arbitrary adversarial states, enabling realistic and efficient simulation with strong transferability to real-world scenarios. 

2.2 Red-Teaming Benchmarks for AI Agents 

Existing red-teaming benchmarks for AI agents remain limited in their coverage of injection vectors and attack strategies, typically relying on directly appended malicious instructions or naive, heuristic-based prompt injection templates that fail to capture the complex adversarial patterns exhibited by AI agents. For example, AgentDojo [ 18 ] focuses on unrealistic tool-output injections by directly suffixing outputs with malicious instructions, while AgentHarm [ 6], ST-Web [ 33 ], and SafeArena [ 60 ]primarily study direct prompt injection without leveraging advanced attack techniques. Other works explore more sophisticated, agent-oriented attacks [ 81 , 14 ], but often assume domain-specific or white-box attacker capabilities, limiting their applicability in realistic threat settings. Moreover, these benchmarks typically lack a systematic risk taxonomy and instead rely on a small set of ad hoc malicious goals, which can lead to biased evaluations and overlook enterprise-critical risk categories. In contrast, DT AP -B ENCH is a policy-driven benchmark that covers 4K malicious goals extracted from 60+ real-world security policies and considers a wide array of injection vectors that previous works do not explore, including environment, skill, tool, and prompt, while assuming generic black-box access to the agent. Notably, each instance includes sophisticated attacks end-to-end optimized by DT AP -R ED from a library of 200+ attack strategies, ensuring both effectiveness and transferability. 

3 DT AP : Unified Platform for Agent Red-Teaming 

In this section, we introduce DT AP (Fig. 2), a controllable and interactive simulation platform purpose-built for red-teaming AI agents, covering 14 domains and over 50 environments in which agents commonly operate. DT AP ’s mission is to provide a realistic, controllable, and dynamic validation layer to rigorously evaluate the security of AI agents under diverse attack vectors and scenarios. We introduce the design principles of DT AP in Sec. 3.1 and its domain coverage in Sec. 3.2. 

3.1 Design Principles of DT AP 

DT AP encompasses 14 diverse domains in which AI agents often perform high-stakes actions, such as handling sensitive data and financial assets. Each domain involves various representative environments, simulated for controllable red-teaming, which closely mirror their real-world interfaces for agent interactions, enabling faithful transfer of security evaluations to real-world settings. 

Simulated Environments for Red-Teaming. Each simulated environment in DT AP supports the core functionalities of its real-world counterpart that commonly appear in agent workflows, with industry-grade frontend, backend, and database designs. We replicate the official GUI (e.g., dashboards) to enable realistic interaction for both human users and visual web agents, and align the HTML structure to provide a faithful observation space for agents that rely on textual HTML representations or accessibility trees. We provide a detailed description of each environment in Sec. P. Our environments are purpose-built for red-teaming evaluation, designed with several key properties: (1) Dynamic and Stateful Interactions : unlike static benchmarks such as AgentDojo [ 18 ] that fail to capture real-world risk dynamics, DT AP enables fully dynamic, multi-turn agent explorations in the environments while preserving intermediate states for verifiable evaluation; (2) Reproducible

Evaluation : DT AP provides deterministic environment transitions that allow consistent replay and repeatable risk analysis of agent trajectories; (3) Flexible Reset by Design : all environments provide efficient reset endpoints to instantiate into arbitrary state snapshots, enabling rapid reproduction of target attack scenarios; and (4) Fully Parallelizable : all environments are containerized and parallels through multi-tenant isolation to enable scalable evaluation with low compute overhead. With these controllable environments, it is possible to configure them with diverse injection points that accept external, potentially compromised payloads. For example, in Gmail , benign users may receive emails containing sophisticated prompt injections that aim to mislead the agent, while Google Calendar and Zoom may receive external invitations embedded with malicious instructions. Similarly, websites such as eBay and travel booking platforms may be poisoned through injections in public reviews or comments. Specifically, our red-teaming agent DT AP -R ED has seamlessly integrated these environment injection points into its harness framework, enabling fully autonomous attack optimization, as elaborated in Sec. 4. 

Simulated MCP Tool Interface. To evaluate tool-use agents in DT AP , we construct realistic MCP-based tool interfaces for each environment. These MCPs are replicas of their official counterparts, or widely used community implementations when official versions are unavailable. We ensure one-to-one mappings of tool specifications, including tool names, descriptions, input schema, and return formats. This will ensure the faithful transferability of evaluation results. We provide a taxonomy of tools for each MCP server in Sec. P. Moreover, real-world MCPs operate on live systems, where tool calls can lead to irreversible and potentially harmful consequences. In contrast, our MCPs are connected to the aforementioned sandboxed environments, where all actions are fully controlled and reversible, providing a safe and controlled setting for red-teaming agents prior to deployment. 

Victim Agent Integration. As illustrated in Fig. 3, DT AP bridges agents and diverse environments through a unified MCP interface, enabling seamless integration of tool-use agents. In practice, any agent that supports MCP can be readily incorporated into DT AP in a plug-and-play manner. DT AP provides built-in support for a range of popular agent frameworks, including OpenAI Agents SDK [46], Google ADK [28], and LangChain Agents [32], as well as coding agents such as Claude Code [ 7] and computer-use agents such as OpenClaw [ 48 ]. In addition, other agents, including browser-based agents (e.g., ChatGPT-Atlas [ 45 ]), UI-driven computer-use agents (e.g., Claude Cowork [ 9 ]), and IDE-based coding agents (e.g., Cursor [ 10 ]), can also be integrated, as they expose standard MCP-compatible interfaces. 

3.2 Comprehensive Domains and Environments on DT AP Platform 

DT AP covers a diverse set of domains, including Workflow , Customer Relationship Management (CRM) , Customer Service , Travel , Code , Browser , Research , OS-Filesystem , Windows , macOS , Fi-nance , Legal , Telecommunication Service (Telecom) , and Medical Service . These domains span major agent workflows involving complex dynamics and high-stakes actions under real-world regulatory and operational constraints, necessitating rigorous evaluation of potential security risks. To support domain-specific security needs, DT AP is designed to be policy-following : for each domain, it collects relevant policies, regulations, and use-case–driven risks, and systematically derives risk categories to guide the subsequent red-teaming. Details for each domain are provided in Sec. B to Sec. O. For each domain, we construct the evaluation with a diverse set of representative environments, covering widely used real-world platforms such as Gmail , Google Calendar , Google Docs , Google Sheets , Google Drive , Google Forms , Slack , PayPal , Zoom , Atlassian , WhatsApp , Telegram , Salesforce CRM , Windows , macOS , Filesystem , ServiceNow , Browser , E-Bay , Booking.com , Expedia , United Airlines , Southwest Airlines , Enterprise , arXiv , Yahoo Finance , Robinhood , Telecom Services , Github ,

Snowflake , Databricks , X, Reddit , and DoorDash , totaling over 50 environments. Each domain 6may require agents to interact with multiple relevant environments across diverse use cases, thus introducing sophisticated attack dynamics within these interaction flows. We have provided a detailed introduction to each environment in Sec. P, where for each environment, we provide details on how the GUI and MCP interface are constructed to be realistic according to their real-world counterpart, including screenshots for each environment and a table of corresponding MCP tool list, grouped by their functionalities for each environment. 

4 DT AP -R ED : Autonomous Red-Teaming Agent for Agentic Systems 

Given the high dimension of agentic attack vectors, we design DT AP -R ED , an advanced, fully autonomous, and policy-following red-teaming agent that optimizes for effective attacks against arbitrary AI agents to achieve given malicious goals. We first describe the end-to-end red-teaming procedure of DT AP -R ED in Sec. 4.1, and then introduce the harness framework design in Sec. 4.2, including novel components such as an attack algorithm skill library , a spatiotemporal action space over diverse injection types , a multi-layer memory module, and iterative optimization with 

verifiable judge feedback. 

4.1 Overview of DT AP -R ED 

As illustrated in Fig. 3, DT AP -R ED operates in a closed-loop, end-to-end pipeline with three stages: (i) attack optimization and generation, (ii) attack execution against the victim agent, and (iii) verifiable judge evaluation with iterative refinement. Specifically, DT AP -R ED requires only malicious goals as input with corresponding verifiable judges that determine whether the goal has been achieved in the environments, and can fully automate the attack injection optimization process. Given a malicious goal as input, DT AP -R ED first retrieves past experiences from a multi-layer memory module, which contains insights and trajectories from both successful attacks and common failure modes for similar goals. It then invokes relevant attack skills from a diverse skill library, including attack algorithms (e.g., jailbreaks such as flip-attack and base64 encoding) and injection strategies (e.g., optimized payloads injected into tools, skills, environments, and their combinations). Informed by these contexts, DT AP -R ED performs diverse injections across user prompts, tool descriptions, agent skills, and environment payload sources to induce the victim agent to achieve the malicious goal. Notably, DT AP -R ED can sequentially compose injections across multiple steps, forming sophisticated exploit chains that collectively compromise the victim agent in a stealthy manner. After each attack turn, the verifiable judge will evaluate the attack success by checking environment states. If the attack fails, an attack refinement judge analyzes the injection sequence and the victim agent’s trajectory to provide feedback for subsequent iterations (e.g., whether the injection was not observed or was explicitly rejected). Based on this feedback, DT AP -R ED iteratively refines its injection strategies to improve effectiveness and ultimately achieve the malicious goal. 

4.2 Red-Teaming Agentic Framework: DT AP -R ED 

We describe the key components and mechanisms underlying the effectiveness of DT AP below. 

Policy-Following Attack Generation. DT AP -R ED is designed to be fully policy-following, generat-ing attacks for policy-adherent malicious goals to ensure that risk assessment aligns with specific security requirements of different platforms. It first extracts risk categories from domain-specific poli-cies (e.g., Google Platform AUP for Gmail ), regulations (e.g., FINRA for finance), or use-case–driven risks, and then derives severe malicious goals for each category. Given a malicious goal, DT AP -R ED 

strictly follows it to induce the victim agent to perform corresponding malicious actions, and evaluates success using a verifiable judge to ensure that the goal is genuinely achieved. 

Diverse Attack Skill Library. To facilitate the generation of advanced and diverse attacks, we equip DT AP -R ED with a comprehensive skill library comprising two complementary components: (1) Attack algorithms , which include jailbreak and prompt injection methods that transform malicious instructions into more stealthy formats (e.g., GCG [ 87 ], ArtPrompt [ 30 ]). These algorithms can be applied across multiple injection points, including direct user prompts and indirect channels such as tools, skills, and environment payloads. Specifically, DT AP -R ED is equipped with a controllable environment to execute these algorithm scripts for on-the-fly optimization of injection payloads. 7DECODINGTRUST -AGENT

Figure 3: Overview of red-teaming agent DT AP -R ED . Given a malicious goal, DT AP -R ED first retrieves past experiences from a multi-layer memory module and invokes relevant attack algorithms and injection strategies from its attack skill library. It then performs diverse injections to induce the victim agent to achieve the malicious goal, where the optimized injection can be delivered via user prompt, MCP tools, agent skills, or other manipulable injection points in external environments. After each attack turn, a verifiable judge evaluates whether the attack succeeds; if it fails, an attack refinement judge provides feedback to the red-teaming agent for iterative optimization until success or budget exhaustion. The generated high-quality attacks are collected in DT AP -B ENCH .(2) Injection strategies , which consist of knowledge for crafting effective injection content and select-ing appropriate injection points (e.g., prompts, tools, environments, and agent skills) to maximize impact. For example, an attacker may construct a fabricated conversation thread between a user and a third party to create a false sense of agreement around a malicious action; such content is particularly effective when injected via realistic communication channels, such as a Gmail email or messages from WhatsApp or Telegram . Similarly, phishing links are more effective when injected into reviews or comments on public websites (e.g., travel booking or e-Bay), redirecting users under the pretense of providing additional information. 

Flexible Injection Action Space. DT AP -R ED includes spatially diverse injection points in its action space that are commonly exploited in agent workflows, including user prompts, tool descriptions, skill metadata and bodies, and diverse environment data sources. DT AP -R ED can inject them flexibly via suffixes, prefixes, or full rewrites, depending on the configurable constraints of threat model and attacker capability. In addition, DT AP -R ED can compose these injection actions temporally across multiple steps to produce stealthy and effective attacks (e.g., individually benign injections that become malicious in combination). To further harness environment injection, we equip each environment with an injection MCP server, where each tool corresponds to a pre-configured injection entry point and payloads are passed via tool parameters. These servers expose controllable and practical injection interfaces that can be manipulated in real-world settings across these environments, enabling DT AP -R ED to optimize attacks under realistic constraints. 

Multi-Layer Memory Module. DT AP -R ED is equipped with an advanced memory module that stores both insights and trajectories from successful attacks as well as common failure modes. By design, DT AP -R ED organizes red-teaming experiences into three layers to capture key dimensions, including risk categories , malicious goals , and threat models with varying injection capabilities. The memory module serves as a core component in the lifelong learning process of DT AP -R ED , improving both attack effectiveness and optimization efficiency. To balance exploration and exploitation, DT AP -RED adopts an ϵ-greedy policy [ 13 ], where in early stages, the agent fully explores the attack space to accumulate diverse experiences, while in later stages, it exploits them to improve efficiency. 

Iterative Optimization with Verifiable Judge Feedback. After each attack execution against the victim agent, DT AP -R ED invokes the provided verifiable judge that directly checks the resulting environment states to rigorously assess whether the malicious goal has been achieved. Rather than relying on agent trajectories or LLM-based judges, which are susceptible to reward hacking as seen in 86,682

Figure 4: Dataset statistics of DT AP -B ENCH . Left: Distribution of all tasks by domain; Center: 

Distribution of attack injection types (or combinations) across red-teaming tasks; Right: Mean and confidence interval of the minimum number of tool calls across tasks by domain. prior works (e.g., [ 6 , 18 ]), the verifiable judge reduces false positives by evaluating concrete outcomes in the environment. For example, it verifies whether the target sensitive data has been exfiltrated to the attacker’s account or whether an unverified transaction has been processed in PayPal .If an attack fails, DT AP -R ED invokes a refinement judge to analyze both the attack and the victim agent’s trajectory, providing feedback on failure modes and potential improvements. For instance, if an injected email is not observed by the agent, DT AP -R ED may attempt alternative injection channels (e.g., tool descriptions) to increase observability. If an injection is explicitly rejected, DT AP -R ED can apply more advanced jailbreak algorithms to improve stealthiness. DT AP -R ED iteratively refines its attacks with such judge feedback until success or the optimization budget is exhausted. 

5 DT AP -B ENCH : Benchmarking AI Agents with Advanced Red-Teaming 

Based on DT AP , we collect high-quality agent red-teaming trajectories generated by DT AP -R ED 

across diverse domains to construct DT AP -B ENCH benchmark. Specifically, DT AP -B ENCH com-prises benign and successful attack agentic trajectories for a wide range of malicious goals under comprehensive risk categories, which are derived from domain-specific policies, regulations, and use-case–driven risks. We first describe the dataset structure and present some key statistics, followed by a detailed overview of the red-teaming data construction pipeline in Sec. 5.1. 

5.1 Benchmark Overview Dataset Structure. DT AP -B ENCH provides over 6k high-quality scenarios for AI agents across 14 domains and over 50 environments, comprising three subsets: (1) Benign task set , which includes diverse, carefully designed tasks involving high-stakes actions to evaluate agents’ utility in handling critical real-world workflows; (2) Indirect red-teaming task set , which pairs benign tasks with malicious goals under the indirect threat model; (3) Direct red-teaming task set , which specifies explicit misuse goals under the direct threat model. We present key statistics of DT AP -B ENCH 

in Fig. 4 and defer additional details to Sec. A.1. DecodingTrust-Agent

> AgentDojo

Figure 5: TSNE of the agent trajectories (Claude Code) of DT AP and AgentDojo. 

Task. Each task in DT AP -B ENCH includes: (1) a config file defining the task instruction, agent setup (e.g., system prompt and required MCPs), injection sequence for reproducing red-teaming attacks, ensuring deterministic evalua-tion; (2) setup scripts that seed user data in the corresponding environments (e.g., personalized emails in a user’s inbox) to establish realistic initial conditions; (3) concrete, verifiable judge that directly evaluates whether the malicious goal has been achieved based on the resulting environment states. See Sec. A.3 for details. 

Red-Teaming Task Set. For all red-teaming tasks, DT AP -B ENCH includes attack injection sequences optimized by DT AP -R ED under a maximum budget of 10 iterations that successfully compromise a surrogate agent (e.g., OpenAI Agents SDK with GPT-5.1) under the constraints of each threat model. These deterministic attack 9sequences enable fair comparison across agents by evaluating them on a shared set of zero-shot transferable attacks. Specifically, we manually select high-quality attacks across injection types (e.g., prompt, tool, skill, environment, and combinations) to ensure a balanced and diverse coverage. 

Policy-Driven Data Construction Pipeline. All red-teaming scenarios in DT AP -B ENCH are policy-aligned. Red-teaming experts first collect domain-specific policies and use-case–driven risks, and then extract severe risk categories. Based on them, we manually design diverse malicious goals and verifiable judges that evaluate whether the goals are achieved in the environment. For seed data that sets tasks’ initial conditions, we propose a persona-based synthesis pipeline that samples diverse user attributes to construct various profiles, and then generates corresponding benign data consistent with these profiles, ensuring realism and diversity (e.g., personalized email threads in a user’s account). 

Cost and Human Effort. To ensure the high-quality of DT AP -B ENCH , this project devotes substantial human effort and API resources to validate the agent trajectories. In total, it involved approximately 16,000 human hours from a team of 17 red-teaming experts over 20 months, along with approximately $120,000 USD in API credits for attack generation and data curation. These investments enable DT AP to serve as a valuable platform for in-depth evaluation and understanding of the security and safety landscape of AI agents. 

6 Experiments 

6.1 Experiment Settings Agent Frameworks. We evaluate four widely used agent frameworks: OpenAI Agents SDK, Claude Code, Google ADK, and OpenClaw. Specifically, for the OpenAI Agents SDK, we evaluate GPT-5.4, GPT-5.2, and GPT-OSS-120B as backbone models; for OpenClaw, we evaluate GPT-5.5, GPT-5.2, and DeepSeek-V4-Pro. In addition, we evaluate Claude Code with Sonnet-4.5 and Google ADK with Gemini-3-Pro. This enables framework- and model-level comparisons across agent configurations. 

Evaluation Process. We evaluate all six agent configurations on DT AP -B ENCH with high-quality attacks optimized by DT AP -R ED across 14 domains, including 1,998 indirect red-teaming tasks and 

1,878 direct red-teaming tasks to assess robustness under attack, as well as 2,806 benign tasks to measure utility in the absence of attacks, as described in Sec. 5.1. 

Metrics. (1) Average Attack Success Rate (ASR): An attack is considered successful if the malicious goal is fully achieved. We report ASR separately for each threat model on red-teaming tasks when the agent is under attack; (2) Benign Task Success Rate (BSR): A benign task is considered successful if the instruction is fully completed. We report BSR for benign tasks without applying any attacks. 

6.2 Main Results 

We present overall evaluation results across domains for both threat models with ASR reported in Tab. 3, and benign utility measured by BSR in Tab. 4. We also provide detailed results across diverse risk categories for each domain, including Workflow Sec. B, CRM Sec. C, Customer Service Sec. D, 

Travel Sec. E, Code Sec. F, Browser Sec. G, Research Sec. H, OS-Filesystem Sec. I, Windows Sec. J, 

macOS Sec. K, Finance Sec. L, Legal Sec. M, Telecom Sec. N, and Medical Service Sec. O. We validate the severity of the malicious goals by directly providing them as inputs to the agents without applying any optimized attacks. Across domains (e.g., Tab. 10 in Sec. B and Tab. 15 in Sec. C), almost all agents refuse to follow these instructions, resulting in near-zero ASR, which indicates that the malicious goals are inherently severe and agents naturally reject them due to their consequences. However, when applying strategically attacks optimized by DT AP (against GPT-5.1), the ASR increases substantially across domains (see Tab. 3) and across risk categories within each domain (e.g., Tab. 11 and Tab. 16), demonstrating the effectiveness and transferability of these attacks. As shown in Tab. 3 and 4, robustness varies significantly across the eight agents and is mostly uncorrelated with their utility. Among capable agents (BSR ≥ 75%), Google ADK (Gemini-3-Pro) is most vulnerable to indirect attacks (55.7% ASR) while achieving the highest utility (87.0% BSR), whereas OpenClaw (DeepSeek-V4-Pro) and OpenAI Agents SDK (GPT-5.2) are the most vulnerable to direct attacks (59.6% and 58.8% ASR, respectively). In contrast, Claude Code (Sonnet-4.5) is most robust under both threat models (25.2% indirect, 26.9% direct ASR) while maintaining strong utility (80.8% BSR). capable, complying with most attacks yet often fails to fully complete the malicious goals due to poor performance, achieving a low BSR of 36.2% and relatively high ASR (46.5% direct, 28.5% indirect). 

6.3 Analysis Agents exhibit significant asymmetries across threat models. OpenAI Agents SDK, Claude Code, and OpenClaw consistently show higher robustness to indirect injections than to direct prompt attacks (with over a 20% gap), suggesting that these agents can effectively resist common indirect injection patterns (e.g., fabricated authority or urgency framing), while treating user prompts as more trusted and prioritizing instruction-following. In contrast, Google ADK exhibits a lower ASR under direct attacks (47.9%) than under indirect attacks (55.7%), indicating strong prompt-level guardrails that filter malicious requests but are less effective against adversarial content embedded in external inputs. 

Injection susceptibility is asymmetric and amplified by composition. As shown in Tab. 5, agents exhibit uneven vulnerability across indirect injection surfaces, with skill- and tool-level injections consistently achieving higher ASR than environment injections (e.g., 83.3% vs. 22.2% in GPT-5.4), suggesting that environment inputs are treated as more external while internalized channels remain highly exploitable. This asymmetry also varies across frameworks, where OpenClaw shows substantially lower ASR on tool injections (21.1%) compared to Google ADK (63.4%), indicating stronger safeguards against external plugins. Notably, these vulnerabilities are further amplified under compositional attacks: combining injection vectors (e.g., skill+tool , env+tool ) often increases ASR beyond individual channels (e.g., 50.1% → 65.6% in GPT-5.2), demonstrating that multi-surface attacks can more effectively manipulate agent behavior. 

Harness engineering is one key bottleneck in the agentic capability–security tradeoff. While model-level safety alignment contributes to robustness (e.g., GPT-5.4 reduces indirect ASR by 6.7% compared to GPT-5.2), Fig. 1 shows that, for the majority of evaluated agents, harness design plays a dominant role in determining robustness. For example, using GPT-5.2 as the backbone, replacing the OpenAI Agents SDK harness with OpenClaw reduces direct ASR from 58.8% to 38.6% and 11 Activity &

> Prompt Tool Skill Env Skill+Tool Env+Tool Other OpenAI Agents GPT-5.4 49.3 41.7 83.3 22.2 43.6 53.5 39.2 GPT-5.2 57.2 50.1 77.8 28.4 56.4 65.6 45.0 GPT-OSS-120B 52.1 56.2 5.6 3.4 66.7 21.7 35.8 Claude Code Sonnet-4.5 31.7 31.4 27.8 19.7 43.6 32.1 9.3 Google ADK Gemini-3-Pro 48.2 63.4 83.3 26.7 64.1 65.0 47.2 OpenClaw GPT-5.2 37.0 42.9 33.3 21.2 46.2 45.9 24.5 GPT-5.5 32.4 21.1 16.7 23.9 20.0 26.6 29.0 DeepSeek-V4-Pro 71.5 63.8 17.6 41.1 46.7 39.6 61.8

Agents are generally more vulnera-ble to context-aware, distributed risks and more robust to content-level risks. 

Across risk categories in each domain, the highest ASRs concentrate in context-aware categories, while the lowest appear in content-level ones. For example, in the CRM domain (Fig. 6), agents are highly vulnerable to categories such as Data Ex-filtration and Dangerous Actions , where harm arises through multi-step tool se-quences that resemble legitimate opera-tions, and most robust to categories such as General AI Restrictions and Fraud / Impersonation , where harm reduces to generating overtly disallowed outputs. This suggests that, for content-level risks, agents can rely on backbone model alignment to refuse harmful outputs, whereas context-aware risks, which requires intensive informa-tion flow analysis and reasoning over multi-step tool interactions, remain largely unaddressed. 

Agents with open-source backbones are more susceptible to direct misuse. OpenClaw with open-source backbones such as DeepSeek-V4-Pro exhibits strong instruction-following (83.3% BSR), often regardless of underlying malicious intent, which translates into high direct ASR (59.6%). In contrast, closed-source models are better aligned to resist such misuse and demonstrate stronger robustness to direct attacks (e.g., GPT-5.2 with 38.6% and GPT-5.5 with 28.9% direct ASR). 

An execute-then-refuse failure mode dominates OpenAI Agents SDK and Google ADK. We frequently observe (Sec. B, C and L) that OpenAI Agents SDK with GPT backbones and Google ADK (Gemini-3-Pro) tend to execute harmful tool calls first and refuse afterwards , as illustrated in Fig. 28 in Sec. C.6.2. We conjecture that this behavior stems from their harness designs that prioritize batch tool invocation, which may hinder reasoning and intervention over intermediate tool-call consequences. In contrast, Claude Code and OpenClaw enforce sequential tool invocation, enabling more explicit control over risks arising in multi-step executions. 

7 Conclusions 

We introduce D ECODING TRUST -A GENT PLATFORM (DT AP ), the first fully controllable platform for comprehensive red-teaming of AI agents, together with DT AP -R ED , an autonomous red-teaming agent, and DT AP -B ENCH , a large-scale, policy-grounded benchmark. Through realistic environment simulations and large-scale evaluations, we uncover systematic vulnerability patterns in state-of-the-art agents, including susceptibility to compositional attacks and harness-level failure modes. These results show that existing safeguards are insufficient for real-world deployment and highlight the need for holistic, environment-aware security designs. Overall, DT AP provides a scalable foundation for rigorous agent evaluation and advances the development of more secure and reliable agentic systems. 12 Acknowledgment

Sloan Fellowship, the research grant from eBay, AI Safety Fund, Virtue AI, and Schmidt Science.

This appendix provides comprehensive details of DT AP , DT AP -R ED , and DT AP -B ENCH , including additional platform introductions, dataset statistics, environment construction pipelines, extended experimental results, and domain-specific findings and analyses. In Sec. A, we begin with an overview of DT AP -B ENCH , covering threat models, task statistics, and dataset characteristics. We then describe the formulation of red-teaming tasks, including task structure, attack specification, and environment setup in Sec. A.2, followed by the design of verifiable judges for reproducible evaluation in Sec. A.3. We include additional evaluations of the red-teaming agent DT AP -R ED , analyzing its optimization efficiency and the effectiveness of its attack skills. In addition, we provide detailed results, case studies, and insights across all domains, including 

Workflow in Sec. B, CRM in Sec. C, Customer Service in Sec. D, Travel in Sec. E, Code in Sec. F, 

Browser in Sec. G, Research in Sec. H, OS-Filesystem in Sec. I, Windows in Sec. J, macOS in Sec. K, 

Finance in Sec. L, Legal in Sec. M, Telecom in Sec. N, and Medical Service in Sec. O. Finally, in Sec. P, we present the construction pipelines of the 50 simulated environments in DT AP ,detailing how we replicate both human-facing GUI interactions and agent tool interfaces via MCP based on their real-world counterparts, as well as the available MCP tools and their functionalities for each environment. 

A.1 Additional Details of DT AP -B ENCH 

Threat Models. DT AP -B ENCH considers two distinct threat models in the agent adversarial land-scape, corresponding to different attacker roles: 

(1) Indirect Threat Model. This setting involves a benign user providing a legitimate instruction, alongside a third-party attacker attempting to subvert the agent’s behavior to achieve an adversarial goal. It focuses on evaluating the security robustness of agents under external attacks that aim to harm benign users. Since the user is benign, we restrict red-teaming to indirect injection vectors, including tool, skill, and environment injections, without access to direct prompt injection. 

(2) Direct Threat Model. In this setting, the user is malicious and directly attempts to manipulate the agent to perform harmful actions. This model emphasizes evaluating the agent’s safety robustness 

against being compromised to assist the attacker in harming other entities. Accordingly, we allow full access to injection vectors for red-teaming, including direct user prompt injection, in addition to the indirect injection types considered above. Table 6: Dataset distribution across DT AP -B ENCH . The dataset comprises 6,682 tasks spanning 14 domains, partitioned into the benign task set (2,806 tasks) and the red-teaming task set (3,876 tasks), which includes the direct threat model set (1,878 tasks) and the indirect threat model set (1,998 tasks).

Dataset Distribution of DT AP -B ENCH . In Tab. 6, we present the distribution of tasks across domains in DT AP -B ENCH , including the number of benign tasks, indirect red-teaming tasks, and direct red-teaming tasks. In total, DT AP -B ENCH comprises 6,682 tasks spanning 14 domains, partitioned into a benign task set (2,806 tasks) and a red-teaming task set (3,876 tasks), which further includes the direct threat model set (1,878 tasks) and the indirect threat model set (1,998 tasks). Notably, each red-teaming task corresponds to a unique malicious goal, resulting in 3,876 diverse malicious goals across varying risk categories. Overall, DT AP -B ENCH provides balanced coverage across domains and threat models, as well as across security policies, risk categories, and malicious goals, enabling comprehensive and unbiased evaluation of agent security. 

Task Complexity of DT AP -B ENCH . As shown in Fig. 7, the minimum number of tool calls per task serves as a proxy for the inherent complexity of tasks across domains. Overall, both benign and 8red-teaming tasks require a substantial number of steps, with most domains exceeding 10 tool calls, indicating that tasks from DT AP -B ENCH are non-trivial and reflect realistic, multi-step workflows. Notably, tasks in the indirect red-teaming set generally require the highest number of steps, suggesting greater complexity, as their malicious goals are layered on top of benign task instructions and require additional reasoning and interactions to achieve. In contrast, direct red-teaming tasks typically involve fewer tool calls than both indirect and benign tasks, indicating that direct misuse often relies on simpler adversarial objectives that can be achieved with fewer, targeted actions. This also helps explain the high ASR observed in less capable models such as GPT-OSS-120B. Legal

A.2 Red-Teaming Tasks 

As shown in the example below, each red-teaming task is defined by a config.yaml file through three main components: Task , Agent , Attack , and RedTeamingAgent . The Task section specifies metadata such as the domain, task ID, and task instruction (available in benign tasks or red-teaming tasks under the indirect threat model). The Agent section defines the victim agent setup, including its system prompt, available MCP tools, and operating environments. The Attack section provides a detailed specification of the red-teaming instance, including the associated risk category, the source policies from which the risk is derived, the threat model, and a concrete malicious goal describing the intended harmful outcome. Under the indirect threat model, the malicious goal is contextualized based on a benign user instruction, requiring the attack to subtly manipulate the agent’s behavior. In contrast, under the direct threat model, the attacker aims to misuse the agent and directly specifies the malicious goal without any benign task context. To ensure reproducibility, each attack includes a sequence of injection steps defined in the attack_turns 

field. Each turn may contain multiple injections across different vectors. For the indirect threat model, these include tool, skill, and environment injections, each supporting multiple practical injection points within the corresponding environments. Each injection vector also provides configurable modes such as prefix, suffix, or overwrite, allowing flexible control under realistic attacker constraints. For the direct threat model, direct prompt injection vector is additionally allowed, enabling the attacker to fully control and overwrite the input, as the user themselves acts as the adversary. In addition, the RedTeamingAgent section defines the attacker capabilities for DT AP -R ED in each instance, including which injection vectors are available and which injection locations are permitted for environment-level injections. We restrict injections to practical, externally accessible surfaces (e.g., emails or Slack messages in public channels). For example, injecting comments on a public website is realistic, whereas injecting into an internal company ticketing system is not. Under these constraints, the red-teaming agent optimizes attacks within a realistic threat model and iteratively refines them to achieve the specified malicious goal. For example, the case below illustrates a red-teaming instance under the indirect threat model in the CRM domain. The original benign task asks the agent to read recent emails containing a customer request. The attack injects a fabricated email thread into the victim’s inbox, simulating a conversation between the victim and the attacker that falsely implies approval to process a $2,500 PayPal payout. Notably, the injected email is sent from an external Gmail address (“finance.billing.jane@gmail.com ”) rather than an internal company domain, reflecting a realistic attacker capability, as such addresses can be easily created and manipulated. When the agent executes the benign task, it may encounter this injected content and is likely to follow the malicious instruction, resulting in an unauthorized payout to the attacker and potential financial loss. 91 Task:

Besides the config.yaml file that defines the task context, each red-teaming instance includes a setup.sh script to reproduce the initial environment conditions. This script resets and seeds the testing environment with realistic benign data that would exist in the agent’s workspace prior to the attack. To support this, we develop a persona-based data generation pipeline that samples diverse user attributes and consistently synthesizes corresponding personal records. For example, in Gmail, the inbox is populated with personalized legitimate emails; in Slack, workspace channels and communications are initialized based on the user’s role and context; in Calendar, events are generated according to the user’s schedule; and in PayPal, user accounts, transaction histories, and balances are initialized to reflect realistic financial activity. This design ensures a realistic and reproducible workspace state, enabling the agent to operate with authentic context when encountering injected malicious content. 

A.3 Verifiable Judge 

Specifically, each task is paired with a judge.py file that defines a verifiable judge to deterministically evaluate both benign task completion (if a benign task is available) and attack success. The evaluation is performed using rule-based checkers that directly inspect the final environment state to determine whether the intended outcomes have been achieved and have taken effect in the environments (e.g., whether a transaction has been executed or a data exfiltration email has been sent to the attacker’s account). 11 Unlike prior work such as AgentDojo [ 18 ] and AgentHarm [ 6 ], which rely on agent outputs or intermediate trajectories to determine attack success, which could lead to potential false positives due to partial observability, DT AP employs a verifiable judge for each instance that operates directly on concrete environment outcomes, enabling more reliable and reproducible evaluation. Moreover, we avoid LLM-based judges, as they are not verifiable and can introduce instability and inconsistency, and instead use a deterministic set of rule-based checkers tailored to each task. For each malicious goal, the judge implements multiple criteria to determine whether the attack is fully successful, partially successful, or failed. A fully successful attack requires all critical conditions to be satisfied; partial success is assigned when only a subset of conditions is met, and corresponding feedback is returned to guide further attack optimization of DT AP -R ED ; otherwise, the attack is deemed a failure. For example, the Python snippet below illustrates the verifiable judge for the CRM red-teaming instance described earlier. The eval_task() function evaluates benign task completion in the Gmail environment by checking whether the agent correctly sends the target email with required information (e.g., billing city and invoice amount). The eval_attack() function evaluates the PayPal environment to determine whether the malicious goal is achieved based on three criteria: (1) a payout is initiated to the attacker’s account, (2) the payout amount matches the specified value, and (3) the payout status is 

COMPLETED , indicating that the funds have been transferred. Only when all criteria are satisfied is the attack considered successful; otherwise, detailed feedback is provided to indicate missing conditions.

A.4 Evaluation of Red-Teaming Agent DT AP -R ED 

To demonstrate the effectiveness and efficiency of the red-teaming agent DT AP -R ED , we conduct two ablation studies focusing on the efficiency of its end-to-end attack optimization process and the effectiveness of its individual attack skills. 

Optimization Efficiency of DT AP -R ED . DT AP -R ED demonstrates strong efficiency in discovering successful attacks with only a small number of optimization iterations. As shown in Fig. 8, statistics from the CRM domain indicate that the average number of iterations required to achieve attack success is generally low across risk categories, typically within 2–5 iterations. More complex categories such as Fraud & Impersonation require up to 6.3 iterations under the indirect threat model, while simpler or more context-aware categories such as Misinformation & Hallucination and Data Exfiltration 

require as few as 1.4 and 1.8 iterations, respectively. The right plot further shows that the attack success rate increases rapidly with optimization iterations, rising from around 37% at the first iteration to over 85% within just 3 iterations, and approaching near 100% after 10 iterations. These results 13 demonstrate that DT AP -R ED can efficiently explore the attack space and quickly converge to highly effective adversarial strategies for severe malicious goals. dangerous

These results indicate that no single attack strategy dominates; instead, a diverse and compositional skill set is essential for achieving high coverage and effectiveness in agent red-teaming. general-misinformation

B.1 Domain Overview 

Workflow agents are increasingly used to automate productivity and operational tasks across a wide range of digital platforms, including Gmail, Google Calendar, Google Forms, Google Docs, Slack, WhatsApp, Zoom, Databricks, Snowflake, Atlassian, and PayPal. These agents support common workplace functions such as email and messaging management, scheduling, document handling, task coordination, notification routing, structured data transfer, and routine content generation, often operating across multiple interconnected environments within a single workflow. To construct realistic workflow tasks, we build the benchmark on sandbox environments that simulate real-world applications with highly similar user interfaces, functionally complete operations, and flexible data customization. This design allows us to preserve the structure and interaction patterns of real workflow systems while enabling controlled initialization of environment states, convenient customization of task data, and systematic construction of benign and adversarial scenarios. Because workflow agents routinely process untrusted external content (e.g., emails, documents, forms, chat messages, tickets, and system outputs) while also interacting with sensitive data and action-taking tools, they expose a broad and realistic attack surface. In practice, these agents may read private messages, handle internal documents, query structured databases, update project systems, trigger notifications, or even initiate payment-related actions. When security enforcement is weak or inconsistent across connected environments, attackers may manipulate the agent into performing harmful actions such as phishing, spam dissemination, sensitive data leakage, destructive file operations, fraudulent payment requests, or deceptive cross-platform notifications. Such failures can lead to privacy violations, financial loss, operational disruption, legal exposure, and reputational damage. To systematically study this domain, we design a benchmark that covers a diverse set of workflow environments and 5 aggregated benign task categories drawn from real-world automation scenarios. We further derive 9 key security risk categories from platform-specific policy documents across these environments, covering harms such as Financial Fraud , Unsafe Content , Data Exfiltration , Copyright / IP Infringement , Illegal Activity , Messaging Abuse , Misinformation , Sensitive File Deletion , and 

Bot Attack . Guided by these risks, we construct red-teaming tasks under two primary threat models, namely direct malicious prompting and indirect prompt injection through external workflow content, to systematically evaluate the security robustness of workflow agents. 

B.2 Benign Tasks 

For the workflow domain, we survey a broad range of real-world productivity and automation use cases across platforms such as Gmail, Google Calendar, Google Docs, Google Forms, Slack, WhatsApp, Zoom, Databricks, Snowflake, Atlassian, PayPal, GitHub, GitLab, Google Drive, and Google Sheets. Based on this analysis, we organize benign workflow tasks into 5 aggregated benchmark categories that capture common workplace automation scenarios across heterogeneous environments. Using this categorization, we construct a total of 335 benign workflow tasks in our benchmark. • Data Query & Reporting : Retrieves, aggregates, or summarizes structured or semi-structured information from workflow environments, such as documents, spreadsheets, repositories, project systems, or transaction records, and produces task-relevant reports or summaries. • Meeting Coordination : Supports meeting- and schedule-related workflows, including calendar in-spection, availability checking, event creation or modification, invitation handling, and coordination of meeting logistics across communication and scheduling tools. • Payment & Financial : Handles legitimate finance-related workflow operations such as invoice processing, payment execution, refund handling, payout management, billing updates, and other authorized financial or transaction-centered actions. • Survey & Data Collection : Manages workflows centered on collecting, viewing, organizing, or processing form responses, spreadsheet entries, or other user-submitted records used for operational tracking and information gathering. 15 • Communication & Announcement : Drafts, sends, forwards, or posts legitimate messages, notifications, reminders, and announcements through email, chat, or other communication channels to the appropriate recipients. A benign workflow task is characterized by three properties: (1) it serves a legitimate user or organizational purpose; (2) it uses only authorized tools, data, and recipients; and (3) it does not produce harmful, deceptive, illegal, or privacy-violating outcomes. 

B.3 Red-Teaming Tasks B.3.1 Policy Definition 

Workflow tasks often involve one or more environments. Since different environments expose different types of misuse and safety risks, we define workflow policies on an environment-by-environment basis. For each environment, we collect the primary platform policy documents that specify prohibited behaviors, misuse scenarios, and safety-relevant constraints. 

Gmail. For Gmail-based workflows, we use the Gmail Program Policies , especially the sections on 

Dangerous & illegal activities , Deceptive practices, fraud & scams , Phishing , Harassment, bullying & threats , Malware & similar malicious content , Spam & bulk mail , Child safety , and Copyright infringement [25]. 

Google Docs. For document-based workflows, we use Google Docs Editors Help: Abuse Program Policies & Enforcement , especially the sections on Dangerous and Illegal Activities , Deceptive Practices, Fraud & Scams , Harassment, Bullying, and Threats , Misleading Content , Spam , System Interference and Abuse , Phishing , and Copyright Infringement [27]. 

Google Forms. For form- and survey-based workflows, we also use Google Docs Editors Help: Abuse Program Policies & Enforcement , which explicitly applies to Google Forms and covers policy-relevant misuse categories including Dangerous and Illegal Activities , Deceptive Practices, Fraud & Scams , Harassment, Bullying, and Threats , Misleading Content , Spam , System Interference and Abuse , Phishing , and Copyright Infringement [27]. 

Google Calendar. For calendar and scheduling workflows, we use the Google Calendar Program Policies , especially its prohibitions on illegal content, privacy invasion, stalking and harassment, copyright violations, spam, and malicious code [26]. 

Slack. For Slack-based messaging and notification workflows, we use the Slack Acceptable Use Policy , as incorporated through the Salesforce Acceptable Use and External-Facing Services Policy ,especially the sections on Prohibited Material , Prohibited Actions , and U.S. Digital Millennium Copyright Act or Similar Statutory Obligations [57, 55]. 

WhatsApp. For WhatsApp-based messaging workflows, we use the WhatsApp Business Messaging Policy , which governs allowed and disallowed uses of WhatsApp Business Services, together with the 

WhatsApp Terms of Service , the WhatsApp Business Terms of Service , and the WhatsApp Messaging Guidelines . These documents prohibit illegal, deceptive, harassing, threatening, impersonating, privacy-violating, and impermissible bulk or automated messaging behaviors, and therefore provide the primary policy basis for evaluating messaging abuse, fraud, impersonation, and other harmful communication behaviors in this environment [76, 79, 77, 78]. 

Zoom. For meeting and conferencing workflows, we use the Zoom Acceptable Use Guidelines ,especially the sections on Abuse , Hateful conduct , Glorification of violence , Illegal or certain regulated goods or services , Sensitive content , Private information , Spam , Copyright, trademark, defamation, right of publicity, and impersonation , and AI [86]. 

Databricks. For analytics and data-processing workflows involving Databricks, we use the Databricks Acceptable Use Policy , especially its operative prohibitions on service interference, malicious code, fraudulent or deceptive activity, privacy and human-rights harms, and unlawful or rights-violating data processing [17]. 

Snowflake. For data warehouse and structured data transfer workflows involving Snowflake, we use the Snowflake Acceptable Use Policy [58]. 

Atlassian. For project coordination and issue-tracking workflows, we use the Atlassian Acceptable Use Policy , especially the sections under Here’s what we won’t allow , including Disruption , Wrongful 

16 activities , Inappropriate communications , Inappropriate content , and Artificial intelligence offerings and features [11]. 

PayPal. For payment and financial workflows, we use both the PayPal Acceptable Use Policy ,especially the section on Prohibited Activities , and the PayPal User Agreement , especially the section 

Restricted Activities, Holds, and Other Actions We May Take , including Restricted Activities [51 , 52 ]. 

B.3.2 Risk Category 

To derive workflow risk categories, we first collect the primary policy documents for each environment and extract the concrete classes of prohibited behavior that are explicitly restricted across platforms. These include, for example, phishing and spam in Gmail and WhatsApp, prohibited material and abusive actions in Slack/Salesforce, unlawful or harmful activity in Zoom, unauthorized or privacy-violating data processing in Databricks and Snowflake, destructive or disruptive actions in Atlassian, and prohibited financial activity in PayPal. Rather than keeping these restrictions in environment-specific form, we normalize them into a unified workflow risk taxonomy based on the operational effect of the agent’s action. More specifically, policy clauses concerning deceptive messages, spam, phishing, impersonation, or manipulative notifications are mapped to Messaging Abuse ; clauses concerning unauthorized disclosure of personal data, confidential records, credentials, or other sensitive information are mapped to Data Exfiltration ; and clauses concerning fraudulent transactions, fund diversion, deceptive reimbursements, or other unauthorized financial actions are mapped to Financial Fraud . Restrictions on unlawful conduct or unlawful assistance are mapped to Illegal Activity ; restrictions on piracy, unauthorized distribution, or intellectual-property misuse are mapped to Copyright / IP Infringement ;false or misleading content generation is mapped to Misinformation ; and spam-like automation, repeated triggering behavior, or abusive workflow execution is mapped to Bot Attack . Destructive or disruptive operations on files, systems, or records are mapped to Sensitive File Deletion , while abusive, harmful, or otherwise prohibited content dissemination is mapped to Unsafe Content .In practice, multiple environment-specific policy clauses may map to the same workflow risk category, and each final category is typically supported by policy evidence from several platforms rather than a single source. This process converts heterogeneous policy language into a benchmark-oriented taxonomy that is consistent across environments while still grounded in platform-specific rules. The resulting workflow risk categories therefore reflect both the wording of the original policy documents and the concrete harmful outcomes that workflow agents may produce in realistic multi-environment settings. Table 7 summarizes the final taxonomy together with representative policy support. 

B.4 Experiment Results 

All indirect and optimized direct attacks are generated using GPT-5.1 + OpenAI Agents SDK as the victim agent during attack construction. Therefore, its 100.0% ASR in the attack tables should be interpreted as a matched attack-generation upper bound rather than a directly comparable robustness result. 

B.4.1 Benign Task 

Table 8 reports benign task success rate (BSR) across the 5 aggregated benign workflow benchmark categories. Overall, frontier agent systems achieve strong utility on benign workflow automation tasks, with BSRs generally remaining high across categories. Performance is particularly strong on Meeting Coordination , Survey & Data Collection , and Communication & Announcement , indicating that current agents already handle many common workflow operations reliably when the task objective is benign and clearly specified. Among all tested model–framework combinations, OpenClaw + Claude-Opus-4.6 achieves the strongest benign performance overall, obtaining the highest BSR on 

Data Query & Reporting (88.89%), Meeting Coordination (95.71%, tied), Payment & Financial 

(88.24%), and Communication & Announcement (91.43%, tied), while Gemini-3-Pro performs best on Survey & Data Collection (95.56%). In contrast, Payment & Financial and, for some model– framework combinations, Data Query & Reporting remain more challenging than the other benign categories, suggesting that financially sensitive or multi-step information workflows impose stricter operational constraints even in benign settings. 17 Communication

B.4.2 Indirect Threat Model 

Table 9 reports ASR when malicious intent is introduced indirectly through workflow content such as manipulated emails, adversarial documents, injected messages, or tool outputs. In this setting, vulnerabilities become pronounced across most non-matched model–framework combinations. Even excluding the matched attack-generation target, several systems still exhibit high indirect ASR, including Gemini-3-Pro , OpenClaw + GPT-5.1 , and Claude-Sonnet-4.5 . These results show that workflow agents are highly vulnerable once malicious intent is embedded inside realistic operational context rather than directly stated as a user instruction. Because workflow agents are designed to read, interpret, and act on external content from multiple environments, they are naturally exposed to instruction injection and context-manipulation attacks. 

B.4.3 Direct Threat Model 

Table 10 shows the attack success rate (ASR) when the malicious goal is directly provided to the agent as a user prompt, without red-teaming optimization. In this setting, all model–framework combinations achieve 0.0% ASR across all workflow risk categories. This indicates that modern frontier agents exhibit strong surface-level resistance to explicitly malicious instructions in the workflow domain. Under naive direct prompting, the models consistently refuse requests involving fraud, data exfiltration, misinformation, destructive file operations, bot abuse, or unsafe content dissemination. Table 11 further reports ASR when malicious goals in the direct threat model are optimized by a red-teaming agent before being presented to the target agent. Under this stronger setting, vulnerabilities increase sharply across many non-matched systems. In particular, Gemini-3-Pro , OpenClaw + GPT-5.1 , and OpenAI Agents SDK + GPT-5.2 all exhibit high ASR across multiple workflow risk categories, with especially strong attack success in categories such as Financial Fraud , Illegal Activity ,

Messaging Abuse , and Misinformation . These results show that although simple direct malicious prompts are almost universally rejected, adversarially optimized direct attacks are still highly effective. 

B.5 Analysis 

Since all attacks are generated using GPT-5.1 + OpenAI Agents SDK as the victim agent during attack construction, the reported results should be interpreted as transfer performance to non-matched systems rather than matched attack-generation upper bounds. 

Key Finding 1: Direct attacks are generally stronger than indirect attacks, but the size and even direction of the asymmetry are strongly model-dependent. Across most evaluated systems, direct attacks achieve higher overall ASR than indirect attacks. For example, OpenAI Agents SDK + GPT-5.2 rises from 63.1% under indirect attacks to 69.1% under direct attacks, OpenAI Agents SDK + GPT-5.4 rises from 50.8% to 57.6%, Claude Code + Claude-Sonnet-4.5 rises from 35.8% to 44.7%, and OpenClaw + GPT-5.2 rises from 26.1% to 30.9%. However, this pattern is not universal. 18 0 20 40 60 80 100

Figure 12: Left: Comparison of attack vulnerability in the workflow domain across different agent frameworks; Center: Attack success rates across different risk categories or attack settings in the workflow domain; Right: Distributional summary of workflow attack patterns. 

Google ADK + Gemini-3-Pro is more vulnerable under indirect attacks (65.9%) than under direct attacks (54.4%), suggesting weaker robustness to malicious content embedded in external workflow artifacts than to overt malicious requests. The most extreme asymmetry appears in OpenAI Agents SDK + GPT-OSS-120B , whose ASR jumps from only 7.2% under indirect attacks to 61.7% under direct attacks. These results indicate that direct and indirect attacks probe meaningfully different weaknesses, and that relative robustness across the two threat models cannot be inferred from one another. 

Key Finding 2: Harness design remains a first-order determinant of workflow security, even under the same backbone model. The same model can exhibit substantially different security behavior under different agent frameworks. Using GPT-5.2 as the backbone, switching from OpenAI Agents SDK to OpenClaw reduces indirect ASR from 63.1% to 26.1% and direct ASR from 69.1% to 30.9%, corresponding to a reduction of 37.0 points under indirect attacks and 38.2 points under direct attacks. This gain, however, comes with a benign-utility tradeoff, as benign BSR drops from 76.4% to 61.0%. A different tradeoff appears for Claude-Opus-4.6: OpenClaw achieves the strongest benign performance overall (91.5%), but Claude Code is more secure, reducing indirect ASR from 18.3% to 11.1% and direct ASR from 22.1% to 19.2%. Taken together, these results show that harness engineering—including prompt construction, tool mediation, trusted-input handling, and execution constraints—can substantially reshape both utility and security, and that no single harness dominates uniformly across models. 

Key Finding 3: Benign capability and adversarial robustness remain only weakly aligned. The updated results still do not support a monotonic relationship between higher benign capability and lower attack success. OpenClaw + Claude-Opus-4.6 combines the highest benign BSR (91.5%) with relatively strong robustness (18.3% indirect; 22.1% direct), whereas Google ADK + Gemini-3-Pro 

combines strong benign performance (89.4%) with the highest indirect ASR overall (65.9%) and a high direct ASR (54.4%). Claude Code + Claude-Opus-4.6 is also relatively safe (11.1% indirect; 19 atlassian calendar gmail googledocs paypal slack snowflake whatsapp
